Paul had respect to an other thing, when he said that the deep cogitations
of Satan were not unknown to him.

Verse. 25. But that which ye have already, hold fas^ till I come. ^^.

HE warns them to be content with the doctrine delivered unto them from the
Apostles, and to admit no new opinions, but that they keep the same even unto
his coming again, and to reverence it as sacred and holy. Whatsoever
therefore the Roman Antichrist and other Heretics strive to bring into the
Church, which is manifest not to have been delivered to the Churches from the
Apostles, we may strongly reject it, as an other burden wherewith the Lord will
not have us to be laden.

Verse. 26.27. For whosoever overcomes and keeps my works to the end, to him
will I give power over nations, and he shall rule them with a rod of iron and
as the vessels of a potter shall they be broken to shyuers.

HE shows who they be which in the end shall get the victory, not those that
for a time shall stand stoutly in the fore front, and afterward be subdued, but
they which shall fight even to the end of the battle. And Christ does set his
woorks as contrary to the works of the Nicholaites, as also his doctrine is
contrary to there doctrine. For as of true and sound doctrine good works do
spring and flow, so of necessity evil works follow vnpure opinions. The
reward which he sets forth by so diverse figurative speeches, is
notwithstanding but one, and the same common to all the godly: which is the
partaking of him self, so when Christ calls us to take part of his kingdom,
whereof he is the right heir, he gives all things unto us which are his,
foreasmuch as we are made one body with him, he promises therefore a
gouernemente over the gentiles, and that of such power, that we shall rule the
rebelliouse and stubborn with an iron scepter, and shall bruise them in pieces,
like erthen vessels. That is, all our enemies being trodden down, and all the
thretninges of our adversaries, brought to naught, we shall quietly inioye the
possession of the heavenly kingdom under Christ which is our head.

Verse. 18. Even as I received of my father so will I give him the morning
star. etc.

Whatsoever things, Christ by reason of his manhood is said to have received
of his father, he received them for us, and not for him self, he is that
morning star which rising in our hearts, drives away all darckes of
blindness.

And we also as morning stars, ye as the sun it (self by his benifit) shall
shine in his heavenly kingdom, howsoever the devil and his ministers, with
infinite sclaunders and lies oppressing our innocence, do labor to obscure
and darcken our name.

Verse. 19. Let him that has an ear hear what the spirit says to the
Churches.

AT every several admonition he repeteth this, least we should let them pas
without due consideration.

The third Chapter.
Verse. 1. And write unto the angel of the church of Sardi these things says
he.

THE first epistle is sent to the Angel of the church which was at ^ar^y,
whereas first it is profitable to note, that although the estate of that church
was most corrupt, shall it wholye almost deserved to be called dead, yet
Christ does adorn it with the name of a Church, flat against the frentique
errors of certain men, which now here acknowledge a church to be where their is
any thing, which does not in all points please them. On the other side the
papists do not require any thing more earnestly, and with greater contention,
then that we would grant the name of the church to the^e company for certain
hundred years, in which the truth was almost buried. If that for a few,
which it is certain were saved, of that huge mass and multitude, we should
attribute this name unto them, yet never the latter we may say with Christ,
thou have a name that thou live and yet in very deed thou art dead. For
althinges are not by and by right: where we may aknowledge a Church to be.

That has the seven spirits of god, and the seven stares I know thy works.

THis preface also even as the rest, does very well agree to the matter: which
is entreated of in the epistell. For he had to do with a pastor and Church,
which he complains to be dead, and life is given only by the spirit of God.
They therefore which had almost quenched the spirit of God as much as in
them lay ought to have sought to him, which alone does give the same
spirit to those that ask of him. For Christ, from his father sends the
holy ghost upon the Church, as he himself often witnesses. John. 14.15.16.
chapter. Of the (...) of the spirits we have spoken before in the (...) Cha.
us. 4. That he also holds ye seven stars in his han^^ he admonish the
pastor that he could not hitherto have remained in any part of life, but that
he should utterly have died, except the Lord had sustained and kept him by
his virtue and power, from extreme destruction.

Thou have a name that thou live, but thou art dead. etc. Thou art counted
among the number of those which live unto God and have renounced the world,
when as yet thou live to the world and to thy self, and art counted as dead
before God. For if a widow which lives in pleasures, be dead eue^ living as
Paul says, how much more the pastor of the church, (which by preaching of
the word of God, ought after a sort, to breath into others the spiritual
life) may be counted as dead, except he renounce the devil, the flesh, and
all the vain inticementes of the world, and frame his whole life to the
obedience of Christ. And we nevertheless in the mean while so long as we have
a name yt we live, do give our selves over to pleasures, we live to the world,
we are dead to Christ. For except the Image of God be renewed in us, in vain
we boast of the grace of Christ, which is not idle in those, whom it has
truly embraced. Neither ought we to sever Christ from his spirit. The spirit
truly can not but work effectuousely in those, which Christ by the grace of
regeneration shall call to newness of life. This place does teach, what great
danger hangs over the Churches, through pastors that are half dead, when
as the infection proceeding out of one, does spread over almost the whole flock.

Verse. 2. Be awake and strengthen the things which remain that are ready to
dye. etc.

THis admonition does show, that this pastor was not utterly dead and
without all recovery, but oppressed even as with a deadly sleep, and except he
should spedely be helped, he would straitway, yield up the ghost. He cou^selleth
him therefore that he without delay awake out of this slepely sickness, and
that he be careful to call back again into life those, things that were
even then ready to die, as if he should say, that he was so nygh destruction,
that death has eue^ now atached some of his members, and his whole body was
even at deathen door, except he spedely shake of this drousines. That he
commands him to awake, he signifies that nothing is more unmeet for a
pastor of the church, then yt he give him self to slepines, whom it behooves to
watch continually over his flock

For I have not sound thy works perfect before God. etc.

He which before said that he knew his works, now shows of what sort they
were, that is, not sound, but imperfect, and which bewrayed his inward
hypocrisy. For how beautiful soever they apeared in the eyes of men, yet
before God they are found imperfect. But are not the works of the best and
godliest, (so long as they are compassed about with the infirmity of the
flesh) also imperfect? I confess they be. But here I say, that soundnes is
opposed to hypocrisy, and not to perfection, which perfectly fulfilleth the
whole law of God.

Remember therefore what things thou have received and heard and hold fast
therefore and repent etc. He ministers a fit medecin to the disease.
Continual sleep had brought forgetfulness. He commands him therefore to call
to remembrance the doctrine which he had received and heard, and hold the same
and to repent for those things which were committed against it. We are taught
here that forgetfulness of the heavenly doctrine brought forth this death of ye
soul, into which he had fallen, so that it is no meruaill, if where it be
altogether silent, (as it is in the papacy) although there all godliness do
utterly die.

If thou shall not watch I will come on the as a these and thou shall not know
what hour I will come upon thee. They that are oppressed with a deep sleep,
are not awaked without great cries and often repeted. Again therefore he
commands him to awake, for if he go on in giving him self to sleep, it will
come to pas, that the lord will strike him with sore vengeance he not thinking
it. And no otherwise then thieves are wont to break up there houses which are
drowned in a dead s^epe and to slay them, suspecting no such matter, and to
spoil them being slain of all there goods: so Christ threteneth that his
coming shall be uncertain to him, which except he shall prevent with wailing
and repentance, his eternal salvation is utterly lost.

Notwithstanding thou have a few names in Sardy. etc. By these words he
shows, that this rebuke did not pertayne to all the parts and members of the
church, but to the most part. For he confesses that there are a few, whom
he also knows by name, which have kept them selves clean and unspotted
from those heynouse vices. Christ does not contemn the small number of these,
ye he does more esteem two or three which worship him syncerely, then the
infinite multitude of worldlinges, which despise his commandments. It is
better therefore to be one of these few, whose innocencye Christ testifies
that he does approve and allow, them to be of the huge multitude of those, which
delighting them selves in there filthy pleasures, the Lord counts as dead.

Which have not defiled there garments. etc. The reason of this Metaphor seems
to be, that those which have given there names to Christ in baptism, have
put on Christ as a garment, that is, have professed righteousness, which garment
they do filthely defile, with wicked concupiscences and works of darkness as
many, as like the dog to his vomit, and the sow to the mire, do return
to there old fleshelye conversation.

And therefore they shall walk with me in white. etc. He promises to the
godly, that they shall not want the reward of there sound and upright
living, for as they have not been partakers with the wicked in there sins,
so likewise they shall be free from there punishments.

For they are worthy. Because it is equity and right that they which have
lived godly and incorruptly, should receive the reward of righteousness
promised them in Christ. For he does not here reason concerning ye deserts of
works, which eue^ the very papists them selves do not maintain, that they
do justify for there worthiness, neither concerning the merits of congruite,
because all this dignity does depend upon the promises of God, and they are
grounded in his free favor he being pacified in Christ. For good works are
neither ours, for they are the gifts of God, neither perfectly good, in so
much as they be ours, theirfore we cannot deserve any thing by them. Moreover
this is a notable conselation to the faithful, which live in that company
where they see the wrath of God by many men's sins daily provoked, that they
may be of good comfort, that God will not punnishe them together with the
wicked, with whom they have not consented in sin and iniquity.

Verse. 5. He that overcomes shall be clothed in white array. etc.

AS well they which shall become conquerers in rising out of the pernicious
sleep of security, as they which are never overcome of the same drousye
disease, shall receive white garments the reward of innocency and victory,
and the token and badge of true blessedness.

And I will not put out his name out of the book of life. etc. That is they
shall receive the crown of eternal life. But for those, which here do labor
to overthrow the holy predestination of God, we must understand, that two
books of life are mentioned in the holy scriptures, the one of election, the
other of calling of the former Paul speaks in the 4. chapter to the
philippians: the 3 verse. and S. John in this reuelat: chap. 13. verse. 8. and
20. verse. 12. and 22. verse. 27. Of the later, ye psalmist speaks, when he
says, let them be blotted out of the book of life, and let them not be
written with the just. And God says to Moises, I will wipe those out of the
book which have sinned against me. Exo. 32. of which this place may not
unfitly be understood: as if he should say, by there wickedness they have
deserved, that I should wipe them out of the book of life, yet if they shall
repent, I will not make void there first calling. For as perteyning to
election, we are taught by infinite testimonies of scripture, that it shall
remain always unchangeable. But many are called and few are chosen.

But I will confess his name before my father and before his Aungells. If any
man be not ashamed of Christ in earth Christ will not be ashamed of him in
heaven. For he comfort the faithful whom the world has always despised,
ye he promises that he will acknowledge them for his own, before the
Aungells, so that they keep the confession of there faith pure and constant
even to the end.

Verse. 6. He that has an ear let him hear what the spirit says to the
Churches. etc.

WHen as this sentence comes so often, it teaches that these admonitions
which the Lord gives to the churches, are not carelessly and lightly to be
passed over.

Verse. 7. And write unto the church which is at philadelphia, these things
says he that is holy and true. etc.

IN the sixth epistle the pastor of the church of Philadelphia is praised, and
receives comfort against the dangers that were at hand, and hanging over him.
First therefore Christ begins his preface with those things which are most
fytte to confime and strenthen this pastor, that he him self is holy and
true. Holy because that being fully consecrated to God his Father, he does
sanctify the whole church, which is his body: whereupon he is said to be
made to us of God, holiness, righteousness, and redemption, (True) not only
because that he is most free from all liynge and dissimulation, to which all
mankind is subject, whereby also he deserves to be called the truth it
self, but also because he alone has set forth true doctrine, yt this pastor
may acknowledge by whose benefit he has embraced true doctrine, and is numbered
among the saints of God, that is by the benefit of Christ, which is holy
and true. What ca^ the Roman prelates here answer, that they chaleng to
them selves the office of sanctifiynge, that they boast and brag that they
cannot err, that the pope does suffer himself to be called not only holy, but
most holy, and holiness it self? are they not blasphemouse against Christ
which alone is holy and true?

Which has the key of david, which opens and no man shuteth, shuts and no
man opens. etc. Which has full power over the house of David, that is his
Church, which so has granted the keys of the kingdom of heaven to his
ministers, that yet he keeps them to him self. Which has so committed the
government of his Church to Pastors, that he may not in the mean time
abrogate his own aucthorite. For least that under the pretense of the keys,
the Prelates should chaleng authority and rule over the Church to appoint
every thing at there own pleasure, he makes this preface, that he only has
the keye of David and opens and shuts at his own pleasure, as Lord and
ruler, whom no mortal creature can withstand whether he open or shut. For
although he promise that he will subscribe and agree in heaven to the sentence
given by the Pastors, if they shall lose or bind any thing in earth, yet
this is so to be understood, when men bring forth for decree, the Judgment of
God, and not there own fantasies. As concerning the Pastor of the Church of
Philadelphia, he ought to be admonished even by this preface only, that he
by the authority of Christ whom nothing can resist either in heaven or earth,
is called to that function and office in the Church, and by his virtue shall be
helped in discharging his duty, and shall be kept free from violence of all
enemies.

Verse. 8. I know thy works. etc.

CHrist does acknowledge that the works of this pastor did please him, which
might be insteede of a spur, that he might cherefully go forward in the
course he had once begun, for what greater reward ought we to wish? Then
that we may do somewhat that is thanckfull and accepted of God.

Behold I have set before the an open door. etc. Saint Paul used this
Metaphore, when as he would show the great hope propounded unto him of
spreadinge abroad the Gospel in the. 1. Corin. 16. And it does fitly agree,
that Christ does open unto us the gate of the heavenly kingdom, when he
preparethe a way to faith, by the Preachinge of the Gospel.

And no man can shut it. etc. He means that there will be enemies which will
labor hard, to hinder and let the course of the Gospel, but they shall
trouble them selves in vain. For in spite of the devil and his Aungells, a
door is opened which no force of the enemy shall be able to shut.

For thou have a little strength and have kept my sayings and have not denied
my name. etc. An argument of a sincere affection, that almost above his
strength which Christ confesses to be but small: he stood constant and stable
in the true and most free confession of the faith. For not always he who^ God
has endued with greater gifts and fit to the same, has the greatest care to
set forth the Gospel. For you may see many which excel in doctrine and
knowledge of tongues, and yet be very cold, and almost dumb in spreading
abroad the Gospel: when as in the mean time, those that are endued but with
mean knowledge, do labor abundantly in the harvest of the Lord, whose
labors, as here we may see, Christ him self will vouchsafe to bless more
plentifully. This place does also beat down yt false Nicodemites, when as it
teaches ye none other can rightely keep the doctrine of Christ, but those
which never deny his name.

Verse. 9. Behold I will make them of the synagog of Satan to wit them which
called them selves Jews, and are not, but do lie. Behold I say I will make
them that they shall come and worship before thy feet and shall know that I
have loved thee.

HE shows that the diligent labor and constancy of this Pastor had
proceaded so far, that he should by his study and diligence draw some even
out of the Jaws of satan. For he says that even of the number of those,
(which falsely would be named Jews when as nevertheless they were the synagog
of Satan, because by all means they persecuted the Church of God) certain
should come, which should humbly desire and crave pardon for there heynouse
offenses, and should confess and acknowledge earnestly, that he was dear to god
whom they vexed hitherto with so great hatred. For in my judgment, it can
hardly be understood of stubberne persons, which are compelled against there
wills to make this confession. For that ought not to be restrained to few but
pertains to all the wicked which are the synagog of Satan, except
peradventure he would note those, especially of that whole rout which above al
others were most troublesome to this pastor whom he said should at length be
driven to repentance, (...) confession of their sins, when it should be to
late.

Verse. 10. Because thou have kept. etc.

CHrist will not suffer there hope to be in vain, which shall patiently wait
for his aid and succor. For he promises deliverance to this pastor from
most great dangers, which did hang over the world for sin: because his
faith and constancy were proved by many experiments and sufficiently witnessed
to him and to the world.

The words of my patience, therefore I will deliver thee from the time of
temptation which will come upon all the world, to try them that dwell in the
earth. As this pastoure suffered not the doctrine of patience to slide from
him, which Christ requires of his servant, but held it even in the most sore
temptations, and in very deed expressed it in his acts and doings, so Christ
promises insteedee of a most ample reward, that he would keep him likewise by
his mercy out of much more bitter calamities which shortly after should light
upon the whole world. He names it the hour of temptation, to teach, that the
times of afflictions be appointed of God, how long men should be chasticed,
that they should labor in vain to deliver them selves out of the same,
which do not patiently wait, for the end determined and appointed of god.
Moreover he calls afflictions temptations or triales because they bewray men
what they are. For the reprobate fret, and rage, or else despair and always
seek out unlawful means to escape contrariwise the godly do acknowledge the
mighty hand of God, to which they do patiently submit them selves, or if at
the most being overcome with impaciency of sorrows they desire to escape, by
dishonest means, yet they never sum or rise up against God.

Verse. 11. Behold I come shortly.

THis is added insteedee of an exceading great consolation, that he might not
fear that the time of his deliverance should long be deferred, and that
whatsoever battle remains, he might sustain it with more quiet mind. For
it has great force to persuade us to patience, when we know that our labor
shall shortly come to an end. Therefore the holy ghost so often in the
Scriptures sets before our eyes the shortness of our battle, that we may
contend more strongly, and bear patiently our labor and trauaill.

Hold that which thou have, that no man take thy crown. etc. Only (says he)
hold the simplicity of doctrine, and go forward in the conversation of a
sound life: do not so behave thy self, that thou which have hitherto fought
manfully in defense of the Gospel, should now at length even in the last
brunt wax weary, and in being vanquished, should lose the crown of
victory, which Christ our Captain would shortly after have set upon thy head.

Verse. 12. Him that overcomes will I make a pillar in the temple of my God,
and he shall go no more out: and I will write upon him the name of my God, and
the name of the city of my God, which is the new Jerusalem which comes down
from God out of heaven, and I will write upon him my new name. etc.

THey were wont to set up trophies and pillars with inscriptions upon them, for
those which had behaved them selves manfully, and became conquerers in battaill
against there enemies, as well to show them selves thankful to them which
had sustained so great labors for the safeguard of there country, as also that
they might sterre up others to imitate and follow there virtues. But Christ will
make his cha^pions pillars, not in the market place, or in the field of Mars,
at Rome, but in the holy temple of his God, that is in the Church tryumpha^t,
in which they shall be made partakers of all the good gifts which God shall
vouchsafe to bestow upon his Church. And where as the trophies and monumentes
of Princes, either through envy of there successores, or by madness of the
people are often thrown down: Christ promises that this conqueror shall be
fixed in so sound a foundation in him self. which is the very foundation of
the temple, that he can never be pulled out or cast down from thence. Moreover
he does assign three kinds of inscriptions for this pillar. The first of the
name of god, by which it is declared to be consecrated to God. The second of
new Jerusalem which shall descend from heaven, by which he is taught that ye
fredome of that heavenly city perteyneth unto him. The third inscription is
the new name of Christ which is given him for his excellent obedience and
humility even unto death, which is above all names, that in the name of Jesus
every knee should bow of things in heaven and things in earth, and of
things under the earth. By the inscription of this name, Christ will take this
pillar into ye partaking of his glory. Whereby we are admonished yt it is
Christ alone which may consecrate us to God the father, into holy pillars of
his temple, that it is Christ alone that may bestow upon us the city of the
heavenly Jerusalem, and to conclude, that it is Christ alone that may call us
to the fellowship of his heavenly kingdom, we ought therefore valiantlye to
fight under him, yt we may attain so great ornaments of victory.

Verse. 13. He that has an ear let him hear what ye spirit says to. etc.

A worthy voice truly which should always sound in our hearts and ears yt
the flattering voices of this world being conte^ned, we may strongly gird up
our Ioynes to ye battle.

Verse. 14. And to the Angel of the Church which is at Laodicia write, these
things says Amen, the faithful and true witness, the beginning of the
creatures of God. etc.

THe last epistle is written to the luke warm pastor of the Church of
Laodicia, to whom also a fit and convenient preface is made, as unto all the
rest, that is, that these things are spoken of him, which is Amen, that is
constant, and not a colored truth, as S. Paul says, that all the promises
of God in Christ are yea and Amen 2. Cor. 1. which is a faithful witness and
true, and therefore can not suffer halting between both parts. For as he has
truly and faithfully declared the embassage committed to him of his father, so
he requires also such ministers as should imploye all there whole strength in
this, that the dignite and authority of the Gospel, may be extolled above all
things.

The beginning of the creatures of God. etc. That word by which all things
are made, to which all the creatures are bound to yeald there full and perfect
obedience, neither ovgh they by any means to give part to the world, and
part to the^ selves of that obeysaunce which is due unto him. For cursed is he
yt does the work of the Lord negligently, whom we ought to worship and
reverence, with all our heart with all our soul, and with all our strength,
neither to make any excuse, by which we may be with drawn by any means from
his obedience. For he does renounce the obedience of Christ, that does not
wholly submit him self unto him, which does never allow any halting obedience.

Verse. 15.16. I know thy works that thou art nether cold nor hot I would
thou werest cold or hot. Therefore because thou art between both* neither
cold nor hot it will come to pas that I shall spew the out of my mouth. etc.

WE ought to be as S. Paul says fervent in spirit, that is, bent with all
feruentnes of mind, to set forth the glory of Christ. The lukewarmnes
therefore of this pastor and his Church is worthily reprehe^ded, that nether
they burned with yt zeal, that they ought, to have defended ye glory of god,
neither were they altogether cold in mayntayning the same. But Christ
pronounces that he does hate this lukewarmnes, so that his stomach can not
bear meat that has so evil Juice and nurishment in it, but that he would
vomit it up. For that they flattered them selves as though they should be
notable Christians, if they were not professed enemies of his name, but should
profess and acknowledge his doctrine so far peradventure as should be for
there profit and commodity: Christ shows that this is but in vain,
because, except they would wholly frame the^ selves to ye obedience of his
word, they should not remain as his members in his body which is the Church,
but should be cast out with his very great lothing and detestation. In that he
wishes they were either hot or cold, he means not that they do any ways
please him which are cold, but that he is so far from alowing the lukewarm,
that he had rather they were cold, that is his professed enemies. For they do
more hurt to the overthrowing of the faith of the simple which craftily thrust
in them selves under some color and show of religion, then they which with
open blasphemies tear the name of the Christ, of whom every one can easily
beware. It is merueill but Christ here by the spirit of prophecye, has
expressed the state and condition of our Churches under the figure of the
bishop of Laodicia, and nothing is more to be feared, then yt we shall perceive
at length to our great hurt, that these terrible threteninges do also belong to
us. The like metaphor Joshua and Helias used, when they forbade the people to
halt on both parts, and commanded them either to follow God only, or Baale
only.

Verse. 17. For thou say I am rich and increased with goods and have need
of nothing and know not how thou are wretched and miserable, and pore and
blind and naked. etc.

ALL burning zeal was not only quenched in him, which is evil, but moreover
he was infected with a foul hypocrisye, which is much worse. And it almost
always comes to pas, that they which are scarcely any thing at all hot in
promoting the worship of god, and the confession of faith, yet nevertheless,
do boast them selves to be very great prelates of the truth, and most sharp
defendores of the says. As this pastor boasts him self to be rich, and not
contented with this, he^ braggethe that his riches are increased into a great
heap, so that he wanted nothing, but had the abu^daunce of all things, even
to satietie: when as by the judgment of Christ he was the most miserable and
most poor, yea beggarly, blind, and naked. We see how that Christ spoilethe
them of all there goods, which arrogantly challenge to them selves riches, and
the abundance of all things.

Verse. 18. I council the to buy of me gold tried in the fire that thou may
be made rich. etc.

Now least the state and condition of this pastor should seem altogether
desperate and incurable, her exhortethe him to repentance, and first of all to
the acknowledging of this his poverty, that he might find true riches in
Christ, who confesses him that he buy gold of him tried in ye fire, that is,
most precious gould, which is, that he should seek the flowing abundance of
all good things at his hands, which sells it freely without taking any
money, but yet to those, to whom nothing is more precious and dear then ye
grace of Christ. For he that will bye that field in which the treasure lies
hid, and that pearl which of all others is most precious, must not cheapen
like the hucksters, but sell all that he has yt he may enjoy so great
treasures, that is to esteem as nothing all other things, so that be may
possess the riches of the grace of Christ.

And white raiment that thou may be clothed, and that thy fylthy nakedness do
not appear. etc. For he was naked so that his filthynes was open and manifest
to God and his Aungells, until he put upon him the righteousness and innocence
of Christ, whereby his filthynes being covered, he may approach into the presence
of God decently araied.

And anoynct thine eyes with eye salve that thou may see etc. Unto all these
miseries was added so great blindenes, that whereas he was void of all good
things, a beggar, filthy, and naked, yet he could epsy none of all th^se
things, but thought him self to be in most blessed estate. For hypocrisy is
always blind, and by the just judgment of God this blindness is cast before
the eyes of hypocrites, that when they think they may without danger mock
both God and men, they deceive them selves so much, that other whiles they are
dead before they feel them selves sick. And when they walk naked and in the
sight of all men, they think the^ selves to be gayly clothed. Such me^ have
need of this eye salve to anoint there eyes wtall, that they may be delivered
out of so great darkness. This metaphor does signifye that when we are
lightened with the spirit of Christ, we do clearly see all things, or else
we see no more in those things that pertain to eternal life, then stocks or
stones.

Verse. 19. Those whom I love I chastise, be zealous therefore and amend.

THat he may know that there is mercy in store for him, if with earnest
affection of mind he return to God, he shows that this reprehension
proceaded of fatherlye love and not of spightfull hate. For whom God loves
most, he does not only rebuke them with words, but also chastise them with
strypes sometimes. For so does he bridle our wantonness, which if it were not
refrayned with such brydelles, we would run hedlong to our own destruction.
Therefore we are much bound to the mercy of God, that he had rather chastise
us with rods, then kill us with swoordes, which proceadeth of nothing else,
but of his fatherly love toward us. But as touching this pastor of Laodicea,
he requires these two things of him, first that he repent him of his
former sloughtfulnesse and necligens, and secondely that heareafter he be
kindled with a greater zeal to set forth ye glory of Christ, and so follow
David, whom ye zeal of Gods house had eaten up altogether, and all other the
saints of God, which cannot otherwise show that they love God entierly, then
if they be wholly set on fire with zeal of aduauncing the glory of his name.
For as we herd a little before he can in no wise abide them that are neither
hot nor cold.

Versus. 20. Behold I stand before the doze and knock and if any man hear my
voice and open the door. etc.

THe love of Christ toward the Church is wonderful great which is so zelus
and careful for there health, which burn with no zeal to set forth his
glory. The verb in greek is of the preterperfecte^s. Whereby he signifies yt
he has long ago looked yt they should repent from the bottom of their heart,
and receive his grace into there heart. Moreover this place does plainly teach
us, that we cannot rightly receive Christ, before he first offer him self to
be received of us.

I will enter in unto him and will suppe with him. etc. The gentleness and
mercy of ye Lord is marvelous, which so willingly pardons so great injuries
done unto him by his servant, that forgetting all injuries he does of his own
accord offer pardon and in sign of perfect reco^ciliatione, he will gently
come and suppe with him. For men are wont by such tokens to renew friendship yt
is broken between the^. But those who^ Christ voutsalueth to be partakers of
his table (as he voutsalueth all the faithful) he feeds there minds with
spiritual daynties, whereby they shall be nourished unto eternal life, namelye
with righteousness freely given, quietness of conscience, and unspeakable joy,
where with the godly are fed by the holy ghost, whereof also he has given
a most excellent pledge, the holy communion of his body and blood, which when
we receive by faith, we have him everlastingly dwelling with us.

Verse. 21. To him that shall overcome I will grant to sit with me in my throne,
even as I overcome and sit with my father in his throne. etc.

HE that shall continue faithful unto the end and with feruente zeal shall
promote the glory of Christ, him does Christ admit unto the partakinge of
his heavenly glory. And as he him self is set in the throne of majesty of
his father for a reward of his victory: so will he advance them unto the
height of his throne, which through faith shall obtain victory against the
devil and the world. For by faith we are made heirs of God and fellow
heirs with Christ.

Verse. 22. He that has an ear let him hear what the spirit

HE concludes this first visione with this his usual acclamation that this
doctrine of the holy ghost is worthy, which should be imprinted in the
minds of all the servant of Christ, and always be remembered of them, that
it may obtain dew effect in us through Christ our Lord to whom be honor
and glory. etc.

The fourth Chapter.
THE second vision is contained in the eight Chapters following, in which the
state and condition of the Church is lively described and painted out, of what
sort it shall be, from that time forth, even to the end of the world. The First
part of this vision which is comprehended in this. 4. Chapter, conteyneth a
description of the divine majesty and glory of God, in which the faithful
are taught that they ought not to faint or quail, no not in ye grettest
dangers, which are governed by his wounderfull council who above all things
has tender care, once the health and safety, of his Church. And when as all
things both in heaven and earth do celebrate, and set forth the praises of
God, it is not convenient by any means, that the same praises should not sound
forth, in the Church militant.

Verse, 1. After this I looked. etc.

AFter that first vision which principally was set forth to describe the state
of the seven Churches, he says there was an other vision showed unto him,
which did express the state and condition of the whole Church, of what sort
it should be in the world, from that time, even unto the end and consumation
of all things.

And behold a door was open. etc. By these words he shows, that a passage
was made open unto him, to the knowing of these mysteries, which except this
door be opened are so included and shut up in heaven, that they far exceade
the capacity of earthly men. The philosophers have diligently searched and
discussed all things whatsoever are comprehended within the circutie and
compass of the heavens, and with meruailouse sharpness of sight, have perced
even unto the secrets of nature: but as for those things, which are above
the highest sphere of ye firmament, either they were altogether ignorant of
the^, or else if they boasted the^ selves to know ye same they waxed vain in
there own cogitations, and I know not what monsterouse things they have set
forth insteede of divine mysteries. As it is to be seen in Plato, Trissmegistus
and others, which men, while they would measure the secrets of Gods majesty
by the co^pas of there own brain, they wholly despised that one only way,
by which access is given to so great secreates, that is the revelation of
God, which is chiefly set forth and declared in his word. Let us therefore
always retain and hold this principle, that we can not, although with never
so sharp speculations, see those things which are in heaven, except God open
the door unto us. How much more is the boldness, or rather ^ may say, the
Impudency of the scoole men, which although neither by visions, neither by the
word of God, so much as a rifte is opened unto them, yet as though they had
thoroughly seen and known all the secrets in heaven, they dispute and reason
of the incom^rehensible essence of God, and of other mysteries, which I believe
the aun* them selves dare scarcely meddle withal.

And the first voice which I herd, as it were of a trumpet talking with me
said come up hither. etc. Although the door were opened to him in heaven, yet
he durst not enter in, but being called by ye heavenly voice, for such modesty
becomes the servants of God, that they wait for his voice in all things
which they attempt: But in that he says it was like a trumpet, he signifies
that he was called with a loud and a shrill voice, that he might be more
attentive in considering those things which the holy ghost would reveal unto
him. Come up (says he) hither and I will show unto the, those things that
must come to pass hereafter. John was taken up in spirit even into heaven,
that he might behold this vision, therefore with the greater study and
reverence, it behooves us to be occupied in searching out these mysteries, and
to have regard both unto the heavenly wisdom, and to the love of Christ,
which would reveal this vision, to the edifying of his Church.

Verse. 2. And immediately I was ravished in the spirit and behold a throne was
set in heaven and one sat on it. etc.

AFter this voice says he, I was drawn into a trau^ce and my spirit being
taken up into heaven, I beheld those things which were set as it were before
mine eyes. In the highest place of heaven an high throne was set, magnifically
adorned, in which one did sit of unspeakable glory to behold, whom by evident
notes, I knew to be the majesty of God. But here two doubts arise, first that
he says a throne was set in heaven, whereas the Lord does glory in the
Prophet Esaye that the whole heaven is his seat: and also he shows that he
saw the Majesty of God, when we read so often in the scriptures, that God is
invisible. We answer to both, that God is not here described as he is, which
fills the heaven and the earth neither can be seen with the eyes of men, but
for the capacite of our wit, that we may acknowledge him as king and
Emperor sitting upon his seat, to whom all things are subject. God is
discribed here bearing the person a king, whose government and laws, it is
necessary the whole world should obey.

Verse. 3. And he that sat was to look upon like unto a Jasper stone and a
Sardine stone, and there was a rainebow about the throne in sight like to an
enteralde etc.

TO the imbecilitie and weeknes of man, nothing semethe more beautiful, then
precious stones. His countenance therefore was like to the two precious
stones, Jasper and Sardine, of which Pliny says, the one has a certain
waterishe shining, the other a fiery. The raynebowe compassing ye throne and
shining, like the green emeralde, does extol the glory of his throne, above
all the benches of men. And it is very like that by these two figures, the
power and mercy of God are lively expressed. For by that watrie and fiery
beauty of his countenance shininge like yt Jasper and Sardine, it is probable
yt his power and justice are signified, which as it was once showed in time
past, in drowning ye whole world so it shall be made manifest at the length, in
the consumatio^ of the world with fire. The rainebow which was a sacrament of
the covenant which God made with men, does without doubt show forth his
inco^prehensible mercy, which co^passing his whole throne about, in a
meruailouse great orname^t to the same. The feeling whereof although it be
figured by ye cherefull color of the emerald, yet it does exceade infinitely,
the bewty of all precious stones.

Verse. 4* And about the seat were 24. seats and upon the seats I saw 24.
elders sitting clothed in white raiement and had on there heads crowns of
gold. etc.

THat the Majesty of Gods glory might more clearly he set forth, there is
joined unto him the reverend company of 24. elders. For kings are wont, when
they consulte of weyghtie matters, or when they will show forth there glory to
the people, for the greater majesty, to join there counsel unto them. So the
holy ghost, when as he would according to the capacity of man's wit, describe
the glory of God in governing the whole world and principally his Church,
expressed it under such a like figure. And this number of senators, does
signify the whole company of Gods elect. But especially of those, which
having fynished there labors in earth, are taken up unto the fellowship of the
heavenly glory. And in my judgment he alludeth, to that promise of Christ,
by which he promised to his Apostles, which tarried with him in all his
temptations, that they should sit upon 12. seats, and should judge the 12.
tribes of Israel Mat. 19. and Luke the 22. where as the number of twelve is
not restrained to the Apostles only, like as it does not extend to them all.
For at that time Judas had the office of an Apostle, but it pertayneth to all
the elect. And although I will not to carefullye search for mysteries in
numbers, yet the number of twelve here doubled, seems to me to comprehend the
Church gathered together into one body of the Jews and gentiles. The elders
therefore are placed in thrones, that they may allow by there consent, the just
judgment of God, which he will execute upon the world, and also may for ever
celebrate and set forth his magnificence. The aparell and deckinge of these 24.
elders is described, that they were clothed in white garments, and had crowns
of gold upon there heads. White raiment in time past, was the sign and token
of noble men, first therefore it commends the dignity of those whom God by
his mercy has called to the adoption of his children: and afterward the
righteousness and innoce^cie, which they have, not of them selves, but borow,
it of the blood of Christ. The golden Diademes, are signs of victory and
kingly dignity, for they that through the virtue and power of Christ, have
overcome the world by faith, shall receive a crown of him, and moreover
shallbe admitted in to the fellowship of his kingdom. No marvel therefore
though these elders can never satisfy them selves in celebrating the praises
of God and of Christ, when as by his benefit, they are drawn out of so great
calamities, and exalted to so great honor.

Verse. 5. And out of the throne proceaded lighteninges, and thunderinges and
voices and seven lamps of fire burninge before the throne which are the seven
spirits of God. etc.

IT is not to be doubted but yt the holy ghost according to his divine essence
does sit even in the throne of majesty with the father and the son, yet by
his virtue which is spread over all through which he sets forth the power,
wisdom and goodness of God toward his creatures, he is said to proceade
out of the throne where the majesty of God does sit, and his force and
strength is expressed by two figures: of which the one does nothing but strike
a fear and terror, the other is sweet and pleasant. Out of the throne, (says
he) proceed lightenings, thunderinges, and voices, and seven lamps burning with
fire, which are the seven spirits of God, An ensample of either of them is
set forth in the law, and the gospel. For ye law as it conteyneth nothing but
yt which is fearful and terrible, so in time past when it was delivered
forth in mount Sinai, it was painted out by such signs and tokens. But the
light of the gospel is acceptable to the hertes of the faithful, which he
promised should come upon those which sat in darkness and in the shadow of
death, and at length it was set forth to the world, by Christ and his
Apostles, and is daily set forth by the sincere preaching of his word. I
confess that there is also some light of the law, but yet such as has fear
and terror mingled with it, as lightening has: but the pleasant and
harmless light of the gospel is fitly expressed by ye seven la^pes. The
number of seven is ye number of perfection and fulness. It signifies therefore
all the gifts and graces of the holy ghost, wherewith god does beutify and
adorn his Church, and the preachinge of the gospel. And it is no new thing,
that the signs and effects have the name of that thing which they represent
or follow. But wherefore they are called many, when there is but one spirit it
is de^lared before in the first chapter.

Verse. 6. And before the throne there was a sea of glass like unto crystal.
etc.

THe state of this world is in the sight of God, which is expressed by a sea
of glass, for although it seem to us diverse, inconstant mouable and frail
(which is signified by the glassye sea) yet to God which governs it by his
will and pleasure it appears sure, stable, and disposed in the best order,
and more clearer then any crystal, because nothing which comes to pas in
the world, is hid from his providence, and although it be such that the
Church may seem every moment to be overwhelmed and drowned with the waves of
ye same, yet all danger of drowning thereof is far of. So yt although we
think that this sea does rage with surges and terrible tempests, yet by the
providence of God, the welfare of the church is as safe, as though the byldinge
thereof were founded upon a dry and hard rock of crystal, and not as a
ship should be tossed to and fro in a swellinge and tempestuous sea. For
this world and all things therein are governed by the will and pleasure of
God, that nothing can hurt or hinder the salvation of those whom he has taken
into his defense and kepinge.

And in the midst of the throne and round about the throne. etc. He sees
the throne of God to be borne up of four beasts like the seat of kings, or
the triumphall chariot of emperors not that his glory needs any such aids,
but because he does meruailously shine in the blessed spirits which he has
created, in which sense also Paul calls the Aungells thrones in the Col. 1.
chap. Moreover when as here is almost the same description of the beasts,
which Ezechiell has in his first and ^enth chap. It is no doubt but that ye
holy ghost had respect thereunto, that he might show forth the self same thing

Were 4. beasts full of eyes before and behind. etc. Ezechiell understood
those 4. beasts which were showed to him in the vision to be cherubins, that
is Aungelicale spirits, whose ministry God uses in the gouerme^t, of the world
and all creatures, they are showed under the form of living beasts, yt we
may understand that the mouinge and workinge of all creatures does depend
upon the will of God, which works by the virtue of angels that we be not
seduced by the gross error of the epicures, that we should think God to be
so shut up in heaven that he does nothing in earth, when as nevertheless
his providence is extended to the smalest things which he has created, which
this figure of the beasts has fitlye expressed, when as they were full of
eyes before and behind, which notablye paint forth the clear sight of his
providence most sharp of all others. For as these beasts full of eyes, can
see all things for the multitude of these eyes, whether they be before or
behind, or on either side, above or beneath, neither can any thing be hid
from there sight, so the eye of Gods providence, fills al places, for as God
is every where by his inestimable power, so also he sees every where by his
inscrutable providence.

Verse, 7. And the first beast was like a Lion, the second beast like a calfe,
the third beast had a face as a man and the fourth beast was like a flyinge
Eagle. etc.

THe forms and shapes of the four beasts are set forth, that they might
represent the Image of all creaturs which are contained in the four parts of
the world. And although in plants, stones, metalles and in other such like
creatures, the power of God also works, yet it is less perceived of the
ignorant (to whose capacity these things are framed) then in those things
in which there is sense and motion, in which it is manifest that God does
move them. According to that saing of S. Ihon the first Chap. In him was
life. etc. But in that which does pertayne to the particular form of every
one of these beasts, because the Lion holds the chief place among wild
beasts, the ox is most proffitable among the came beasts, the man is ruler
over all living creatures, the Eagle is called the chief of fowls, under
these four shapes this vision does comprehend all other living creatures.

Verse. 8. And the four beasts had each one of them six wings about him.
etc.

THe wings do signifye the swiftenesse, wherewith these Aungelicall spirits,
do carry with meruailouse quicknes, the power and will of GOD to all parts of
the world. The number of the wings does show that Christ had respect to those
things which Esaias speaks of the Seraphines, in the sixte chapter of his
prophecy, which had two wings to cover there faces, two to cover there
feet, and two wherewith they did fly. For when as the Cherubins and Seraphins
also them selves, shall approach into the presence of Gods Majesty, they cover
there faces, as though they were not able to behold so incomprehensible
glory, and they hide there fete as it were, acknowledginge there own
imperfection: finally, being wholly prepared to his obedience, they fly with
two wings. How much therefore is the arrogancy of hypocrites to be abhorred,
which when they be altogether defiled and profane, neither by any means
^ramed to the obedience of God: yet they dare boldly step forth into his
presence, neither do they fear least this impudency of theirs should be
corrected with thunder thrown down from heaven upon them.

And they were full of eyes within. etc. He said before that they were full of
eyes on every side, before and behind, and now he repeteth that not only
without, but also within they were full of eyes, that he may stretchforth the
providence of God, not only to these things which are wrought openly and as
it were in outward face, but that the force thereof does reach even unto those
things also which are within, although never so secreate and removed from the
eyes of man, which thing we shall well understand, if we shall diligently
consider the generation, conservation, form, beauty, order, virtue and
disposition, of all creatures.

And they ceased not day and night. The Angelical spirits have never any
lesure to cease from singing the praises of God, but they celebrate ye same
with perpetual melody. And truly the glory of god which is in all creatures,
ye very du^be stones do proclaim. And what other thing speak all things in
general? then yt the power and wisdom of god which made the^, is equale with
his goodness. Which when it is done of the^ without intermission or ceasinge. I
greatly marvel how it comes to pas, that men for whose sake all other
things were created, should think, that they may always leave of from
celebrating and publishinge the praises of God. But this truly does excede all
monsters, that they which excelled al other living creatures upon earth not
only in exellencye of creation, but also exalted to the dignity of Aungelles,
by the benefit of redemption, should be so far from immitatinge the Angels
and other creatures in settinge forth the praises of God, that they may seem
almost to have conspired with the devils, that they may pull him out of ye
throne of his majesty. Hear but that I do reverence the secreat judgment of
God, which I do not understand, I have nothing else wherewith to rid my self
out.

Saying Holy, Holy, Holy. etc. This is the celebration of the divine praises,
that they attribute holiness only to God, which except all creatures borow of
him, they are profane and polluted. But as I suppose that this thresold
repetition does avow the mystery of the trinite, so I think it is scarce mete
to stifely to urge the same against heretics, as the old fathers did. For the
chief points of our religion ought to be proved with more manifest
testimonies, especially when we have to do with those which are ye enemies of
our faith, to whom we be com laughing stocks, if sticking so fast in weak
proofs, we may seem to have less force to resist them. For it is like that
the continual and unwearied study of the Aungells is noted in this place,
rather then the divinity of the three persons.

Lord God almighty. etc. They extol the holiness, the omnipotence and eternity
of God, not only that he is such in him self, but because he is the
unsearchable fountain of al goodness out which every good thing does slow
into the Church of even holiness, and the perfect fruition of eternal
blessedness. We ought not to imagine a bare and idle almightye power of God
about which yt Sorbonistes do vainly contend, but which may suffice to execute
in all things his most holy will. For as often as god is named almighty, it
is for this cause, that our faleth may be strengthened against temptations,
that we may not doubt at all, of the performinge of his promises, which is
induced with strength and power to bring to pas whatsoever he has decreed.
But his eternal divinity and godhed is therefore set forth, that we may learn
to cleave and stick only to him, when as all other things are inconstant and
uncertain, except they be sustained and kept from destruction, by his secret
virtue and power.

Verse. 9.10. And when these beasts gave glory, and honor, and thanks to him
that sat on the throne which lives for ever and ever, the 24. elders fell
down before him that sat on the throne and worshipped him that lives for
evermore. and cast there crowns before the throne saying. etc.

IF the Angels with all other creatures are inflamed with so great feruentnes
to singe forth the praises of God, that they never leave of the study and care
they have to celebrate ye same, what should the Church do, upon which
greater benefits of God are bestowed, then upon any other creatures? ought it
not to be wholly turned into thanks geuinge? when the Church therefore does
acknowledge, especially the fellowship of those which triumph in heaven: by
comparinge them selves with the other creaturs, how much she owe^h unto god:
She is ashamed that she should be most slackest of all things in celebratinge
the praise of God. Therefore she does prepare her self to holy emulation, and
whith how many more benefits god has adorned her, so much more cherefully she
proceeds forward, to the continual celebrating of his glory. For seeing John
says, when the beasts gave glory to God, the elders do the same, (and he had
said before that these beasts had no rest day nor night from this holy
off^ce), he shew^th that the glory of God is sounded forth of the elders with
continual and vnweried study. But it is worthy to be noted with what
circumstances: the Apostell commends this there diligence. First he shows
that they fell upon there faces before the throne of God, where by there humble
submission is noted: that when they should set forth the praises of God, they
seem to ask pardon, because they can not think or speak any thing worthy
his glorious majesty. For so we ought to come to declare his praise, that we
may acknowledge our selves far unmeet to such an office. Away therefore with
every such proud opinion of desert, whereby men be puffed up as though they
should deserve God, when they come forth to glorify his name, which if they
understood how unworthy they are to take so excellent a thing upon them,
even for this cause they would give immortal praises to God, that he should
vouchsafe them so great honor, as to suffer the^ to be publishers abroad of
his glory. Secondly they worship him which lives for ever and ever. whereby
they profess there obedience to be due unto God, and there duty binds that
they show them selves, thankful for so great benefits bestowed upon them.
Lastly they cast there crowns before the throne, by which sign they declare, yt
they wholly renounce all there own dignity, or which by any means pertayneth
unto them, yt they may render to God his due obedience. For we can not any
other way come into the presence of God to sing forth his praise, then if we
spoil our selves of all our ornaments, that God may have his perfect glory
alone. For all heyght must fall down when we shall come into his presence, and
it behooves all things to appear humble before his face. Moreover we must
note that not to Angels, or Archaungels, or to the blessed virgin, but to him
alone which sits upon the throne all glory is to be ascribed.

Verse. 11. Thou art worthy o Lord to receive glory honor and power.

THey do not sound the praise of God with their lips only or with there
tongue as the manner of hipocrites is, but from the bottom of there hearts they
confess as they have tried, that all honor, glory and power is to be
ascribed only to God* when they say thou art worthy o Lord. We are hear
also admonished that no honor is dew to creatures which deserve none, but to
God only which is worthy thereof.

Because thou haste created all things, and for thy wills sake they are and
have been created. etc. They show that all glory is dew unto god for two
causes, first because he has created all things, by which right aloe wall
creatures owe obediens to there maker. Secondly for the end of there creation,
for all things were created for his will, and therefore ought to obey his
will. This place teaches, that the praises of God ought to be celebrated, not
only with the tongue but with all actiones of our life, which ought to be
framed unto his obediens. Where be then the merits of works either of
congruitie or condignitie (as they call the^) when as even by the very right of
creation, all our service and obedience is hear affirmed to be dew unto God

The fyfte Chapter.
AFter that vision was showed unto S. John whereby was showed the glory of
Gods majesty, that we might know from what foundation this prophecy was
drawn, in the next place is taught, by means of what mediator, the
knowledge of so secreate things could come unto the Church. For the vision
which is set forth in this Chapter does teach, that it is only Christ which
can open unto men the secreate will of his father. For he is the only doctor
of his Church, which has brought the truth unto us from heaven that is of
so great distans from earth, whose voice also his heavenly father has
commanded us to hear.

Verse. 1. And I saw a book in the right hand of him that satte upon the
throne written within and without sealed with seven seals. etc.

THis book conteyneth the whole will of God which is set forth to be known
of men, especially those decrees of his purpose which concern, the
disposition of his Church in this last age of the world, as it is manifest by
the next chapter. Whereby we understand that there be some secreats of Gods
will which because they are not contained in this book of his revealed will,
it is wickedness for men to seake to know the^. I will say moreover they
labor all in vain to know those things which God of purpose would have to
be hidd, wherefore hear must needs fall down a great portion of the scoole
divinity, which is occupied in searchinge out those things only, which God
by no means has vouchsafed to make known unto me^. Moreover we are
admonished, that the decrees of God, concerning the governing of his Church,
are so certain, that they are registered in a book, that we should not think
that any thing falls upon the Church by fortune or chance, or beside the
will of God. And whereas this book us written within and without, it
signifies, that it conteyneth the narration of many and diverse things, which
could not be expressed in two or three leaves, but scarsely be contained in a
whole book written all over. This book is sealed with seven seals, that is
to say it is closed up on every side so that although it be written both with
in and without. Yet nothing can be red thereof before the seals be opened. For
the number of seven as we have showed before, signifies fulness and
perfection. Finally in that this book is seen to be in the hand of him that
sits upon the throne, we are taught that the disclosing of Gods secreates,
cannot be attained unto by man's wit except God him self teach the same to
us by his hand. When God therefore holds the book in his right hand, which
afterward the lamb does open, he signifies, that the knowledge of all
mysteries, does flow from him alone through our Lord Jesus Christ.

Verse. 2. And I saw a mighty Angel, which preached with a loud voice, who
is worthy to open the book and lose the seals thereof. etc.

THat all the world might more certeynly and manifestlye know, that Christ
only is the interpretour of the heavenly will of God his father, a mighty
angel is sent forth, which with a loud voice should proclayme to all
creatures in heaven and earth, if any have any opinion of there own excellency
and dignity, whether they be worthy to open yt book and to loose the seals
thereof.

Verse. 3. And none could neither in heaven, nor in earth nor under the earth
open the book neither look thereon. etc.

THere was not found such worthiness in any creature, that they could either
open the book or see any thing therein. And if the merits of no creature are
so much worthy, as that they may attain to yt knowledge of Gods will: how
much less shall they be able to perform his commandment, and to purchase
everlasting life? That he reherseth the creatures that are in heaven, in
earth, and under the earth, he does it to amplify the matter. For if heavenly
creatures are unworthy, much more vnworthtie are earthly creatures, or such as
are under the earth.

Verse. 4. And I wept very much because none was found worthy to open the
book or to read the book neither to look thereon. etc.

WHen as Saint Ihon knew by inspiration of yt holy ghost, that the
knowledge of those things which were contained in that book, was very
proffitable and necessary for the Church, and yet there was none, found
among Aungells or men which could make him pertaker thereof, he burst out
into weeping, and grievously complained that none was found, which was
worthy to open this book or at the lest to look upon it. The godly affection
of the Apostle toward the Church is here commended unto us, in that he
testifies by his weeping, what desire he had to promote the same unto all
necessary knowledge, so that he wept so abou^dautly because he thought for the
time, that the knowledge of this book had been denied him. What shall we then
say unto those men, unto whom when as the care of instructinge the Church is
committed, yet thy follow there own pleasure, and are so far of from being
grieved at the ignorance of there flock, yt they the^ selves through there
idleness and sloughtfullnesse do nurish among them dame ignorance the mother
of all mischief. And with what spirit shall we say that the popish prelates
are ledd, when they are grieved at nothing more, then if the Church of God
obtain knowledge of those things, which the goodness of God has appointed for
her use. This truly is the spirit of Antichrist, that he should forbid
those things to be known which the holy ghost has delivered to that entente
they should be known. where as S. Ihon testifies that he wept exceadingly,
because those things were kept from the knowledge of the Church for a time
that she might better understand by whose benefit she obtained the knowledge
of them at the length.

Verse. 5. And one of the elders said unto me weep not. etc.

THe Lord suffered not his servant to be long vexed for the want of that thing
which by any means should be proffitable unto him, but when he had first
showed yt all other means did fail, he brings forth our only mediator by
whose means, that whereof S. Ihon did almost despeyre should be be performed.
For they that thrist after ye knowledge of good things (as he has promised)
shall at length be satisfied. Wherefore one of the 24. elders is sent unto him,
to comfort him, declaring that one was now sound, that should open the seals
of the book, read it, and also make the Church partaker of it.

Weep not. etc. As if he should say, this vision was not therefore showed
thee, that thou should vex thy mind thereby with immoderate sorrow but this
rather was the meaning of the holy ghost, to admonish the that this treasure is
opened unto the proffite of the Church by the benefit of Christ alone. Here
let the Church learn worthily to esteem the knowledge of these mysteries,
seeing it is purchased unto her by no other means, but by the mediation of
Christ alone.

Behold the Lion of the tribe of Judah, the root of David has obtained to
open the book, and to lose the seven seals thereof. etc. He reportethe a
matter worthy both to be maruailed and also diligently considered, that
only Christ took in hand and accomplished a work of such difficulty which
could be brought to pass by none other means. Wherefore he calls it his
victory not unworthily, because none durst take ye enterprise in hand but he.
But let us here see with what co^mendations he sets forth the virtue and
power of Christ. First he calls him a lion of the tribe of Judah, secondlye
the root of David and last of all the conqueror of so hard an enterprise.
That he calls him the lion of the tribe of Judah, he alludeth doubtless, to
the old prophecy of the patriarch Iacobe concerning his son Judah: that he
should be as a lion terrible to his enemies, so that even when he was a sleep
no man durst awake him. Now what dignity or prerogative so ever God gave unto
that tribe, it is certain it was for this only end, to set forth the glory
of Messiah which should arise out of that tribe. Wherefore the whole tribe was
as a lions whelpe terrible to their enemies, but especially Christ which was
borne of that tribe, may chaleng this honor unto him, that as a conqueror and
triumpher over all his enemies, he sits in heaven on high. He calls him
the root of David to show that it is he whom the scriptures pronounces to be
the son of David. Esaye truly magnifieth a rodd that should spring out of
the dry stock of Isay the Father of David, upon which the spirit of
understanding, wisdom, counsel, prudence, and fear of the Lord, shall rest
that worthily we may acknowledge that it is he, whom it behooves only to
open these heavenly mysteries unto us. When he therefore with these
commendations, sets forth his fearful power, and wonderful wisdom, he
declares that he only is worthy, which should obtain the victory of so
hard a conquest.

Verse. 6. Then I beheld and ^o in the midst of the throne, and of the 4.
beasts and in the midst of the elders, stood a lamb as though he had been
killed which had 7. horns and 7. eyes which are the spirits of God sent into
all the world etc.

ANd when as one of the elders had spoken these things, by and by (says he)
I saw a lamb in the midst of the throne as though he had been slain which
took upon him to open this book, which lamb he shows by many circumstances
to have signified Christ. First that he did apeare under y^ shape of a lamb,
whom Ihon Baptist magnifieth to be appointed of god the la^be which should take
away the sin of the world, by the sacrifice of his death. But it is meruail
that he was showed in the form and shape of a lamb, whom one of the elders a
little before had said to be a Lion. But thus we must answer: that Christ is
a lion against his enemies, and a lamb that he might yield that obedience
to his father, by which he should deserve to be the redeamer of the world.
Moreover, that this invincible force and strength painted forth under the
figure and shape of a lion, never showed it self forth more happily, then in
the sacrifice of his death, by which he most strongly subdued the deadly
enemies of mankind, the devil, death, sin, and hell: Furthmore the lamb
apeareth in the midst of the throne, that the equalle of the divine glory he
has with the father might be showed. Away therefore with the cursed impiety of
the heretics, which pratleth yt Christ is not equal with the father, when as
S. Ihon saw him in the midst of the throne, and of the 4. beasts and 24.
elders, by which figures the greatest majesty and glory of the mighty God is
described. He seems as though he had been slain, because by the price of
his death, he obtained this in comparable dignity to his manhod, that he might
be worthy which should open the secreate mysteries of Gods counsel to men.
Besides this he has seven horns and seven eyes, by ye horns no doubt is
signified his full strength and power, for although he show him self meke and
gentle, under the shape of a lamb, yet he has might enough whereby he is able
to break and overcome, the force and strength of all his adversaries. The
mystery of the seven eyes, S. Ihon him self expounds, to signify seven
spirits, which are sent into the whole earth, that is the perfect wisdom of
the holy ghost, by which Christ does govern his Church which is spread over the
face of the whole world. Let us not fear therefore to become subject to Christ
which is endued with so great power, that nothing is able to resist him and
moreover furnished with so wounderfull providence, that he guides and
disposes al things, in all places to the safeguard of his Church.

Verse. 7. And he came and took the book out of the right hand of him that sat
upon the throne. etc.

CHrist which is the lamb of God, slain from the beginning of the world, is
found worthy alone, which should open and read this book. Which when it was
testified sufficiently by the voice of an Angel, he could have rested and
been content with that praise which was granted to him of all things, if he
were no rather desirouse of our health and salvation, then of his own glory.
Christ therefore came out of the bosom of his father and out of the middes^e
of the throne, an interpretor of Gods divine will and pleasure, and for our
instruction has opened the book which he took out of the right hand of him
that sat upon the throne, which being opened he expounds it unto us.

Verse. 8. And when he had taken the book, the 4. beasts and 24. elders fell
down before the lamb having every one harpes and golden vials full of
odoures, which are the prayers of ye saints. etc.

WIth what thankefullnesse of mind we ought to receive this benefit of
Christ, we are taught by ye example of the beasts, of the elders, of the Angels
and of all ye creature of god. So much more our vnthankefullnesse is to be
lothe^, which when we are those to whose commodity and health this whole
benefit is referred, yet as though this thing did very little or nothing at
all touch us, we neither show our selves thankful to the aucthor of this
benefit, neither do greatly esteem ye benefit it self. But it behooves us
to consider, by what means they prepare themselves to sing forth the praises
of God. They fall down upon their faces before yt lamb, as well the 24. elders
as the 4. beasts, and do offer unto him divine honor. For it is he who^ all
the angels of God, are commanded to worship. For no creature which is not
God can take this honor, without great sacrilege, this la^be of God therefore
is God whom all the Angels do worship. Moreover by this gesture they
testifye there modesty and submission, that they are no otherwise fit to set
forth the praises of Christ, then if they acknowledge the^ selves altogether
unworthy of so great honor. They have every one harpes, whereby the consent
and harmony might be the greater, by which they show that all there strength,
and the inward affections of there mind, do agree with there tongue in
celebrating and magnifying the praises of God. Moreover they have golden vials
full of odoures, which are the prayers of the saints, as he him self
expounds it. For the Church which is the congregation of the saints,
offers to God a sweet smelling sacrifice, when she pours out unto Christ,
prayers, and supplications for the health of the whole body and every member
of the same. For so yt prophet of old time did sing in ye 141. Psalm. let my
prayer be as an incense in thy sight, and the lifting up of my hands as the
evening sacrifice. And they have these sweet odoures in golden no^els, that we
may be admonished, that those prayers must proce^de out of a pure and sincere
heart, which shall be acceptable in the sight of Christ. Let us offer up
therefore the incense ^f our prayers, out of golden viales, corrupted and
defiled with no hypocrisy or wicked affection, so they shall be for a savor of
sweetness to the Lord and his Christ. But that, certain do here contend yt
the prayers of the saints living in earth are offered up to God, either by
the Angels, or by other saints departed out of this life, it is more weak,
then that it should need any long confutatio^. For here the. 24. elders which
are interpreted, the saints, are said to offer to God not the prayers of
others, but there own, whereby is signified, that the intercessions of the
whole Church are offered to God through our only mediator Christ Jesus, for
we saye^ before that this fellowship of the elders did signifye and represente
the Church, partly warring in earth, and partly triumphing in heaven with
Christ their Emperor.

Verse. 9. And they sing a new song saying. etc.

BY singing they express there joy and sincerity of mind. Confessing that
nothing can be more pleasant unto them, then for ever to magnify and set
forth the praises of Christ. And they sing a new song, the recording whereof
continuallye, is never tediouse or ircksome unto them. Although they shall
sing it a thousand times, yet the newnes thereof does always please the^,
because they do celebrate ye praises of Christ for ever without wearines. For as
the benefit of Christ, is eternal, it so behooves that his glory should also
be eternal. And like as by no age or continaunce of time the excellency of
his goodness is deminished, so the remembrance of his love ought never to wax
old in the minds of the godly. But always they may think they have new
matter offered unto them, to magnify Christ, as often as they shall consider,
that the profit and commodity of his benefits shall never die. Which if it
were taken of all men with that affection it ought: There should not so many
be found, which with full mouths do boast them selves to be the disciples of
Christ, to whom nothing is more tediouse, and fuller of weariness, then to
leave of from there trifels and vain pleasures a very small time, to the
settinge forth of the glory of Christ.

Thou art worthy to take the book and to open the seals thereof because thou
wast killed. etc. This truly is the song, which with heart, mind and tongue
they sing to the praise and commendation of Christ. That the lamb of God is
worthy alone among all creaturs, which should take ye book and open the
seals thereof, which has deserved to open unto men the will of his heavenly
father, which was seven fold shut up and hid from the knowledge of mankind.
They show why he is worthy, when they say he was slain, for the obedience of
Christ deserved so much, that it brought to us (god being reconciled) health
and salvation, to him self the dignity of incomparable glory.

And thou have redeamed us to God by thy blood out of every kindered, and
tongue, and nation, and people. etc. The effect of his death is described,
that we which were the servants of sin, guilty of death, and bound slaves
of the devil, by his inestimable grace are delivered from so great evils our
price and ransom being paid, not gould or silver or any corruptible thing,
as Peter saiethe, but his own precious blood, which has washed us from al
uncleanness, and brings us forth into the presence of God clothed with new
righteousness, not only chosen out of the tribe of Judah, or out of the tribes of
Israel, but taken out of every kindred, tongue, people ^ nation, whereby the
calling of the gentles is plainly declared.

Verse. 10. And have made us king and priest to our God. etc.

WE are made of servants kings, that is partakers of the heavenly kingdom
by says, of profane and unclean despisers of God preistes, to offer up
spiritual sacrifices to God.

And we shall reign upon the earth. etc. He signifies that we shall not have
a bare and empty name of a king, as we know many to challenge to them selves
the kingelye name, when as they have no government or kingedome at all, he
says it shall come to pas that the faithful shall in very deed reign over
the earth. Yet this seems to be against reason, that they seem to say that the
kingedome of CHRIST shall be earthly which he flatlye denies to be of this
world, but we must understand that under the figure of an earthlye kingedome
the heavenly kingedome is described. And the faithful are admonished, in the
mean time to bear all greifes patiently which they sustain for the
professinge of the gospel, whereas shortly after kinglye dignity shall follow
there miseries and calamities, which ought to be unto them insteede of great
consolation and comfort. Let us learn here when it comes to pas that we be
oppressed wc the cruel tyranny of satan or his ministers, not to despair,
but to lift up our minds to the most blessed hope of the heavenly
kingedome. He is faiethfull which has promised, I even now this in heritage is
purchased, if that we patiently wait for the time apoincted of God, when we
shall enter into the same.

Verse. 11. And I saw and heard the voice of many angels about the throne and
about the beasts and the elders. and there were thousane thousands. etc.

Saint John does diligently set forth this vision, that of all creatures as
many as are in heaven or in earth, Christ is acknowledged to be worthy alone,
which should be the interpreter of his fathers will, and declare and open to
his Church, the mysteries of things to come. But it is obscure and darcke
which he says, that he saw and heard the voice, when a voice is only heard
and not seen, but we must remember, that sight is not to be referred to the
voice, but the infinite multitude of Aungelles which compassed about the throne
of God. Like as the glory of a king stands in the multitude of people, so
